<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Rice</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Rice_(disambiguation)" title="Rice (disambiguation)">Rice (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White,_Brown,_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg/300px-White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg/450px-White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg/600px-White%2C_Brown%2C_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White,_Brown,_Red_%26_Wild_rice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mixture of brown, white, and red indica rice, also containing <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a>, <i>Zizania</i> species</div></div></div>
<p><b>Rice</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seed</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grass</a> species <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> (Asian rice) or <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_glaberrima" title="Oryza glaberrima">Oryza glaberrima</a></i> (African rice). As a <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal grain</a>, it is the most widely consumed <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> for a large part of the world's human population, especially in Asia. It is the agricultural commodity with the third-highest worldwide production (rice, 741.5 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> in 2014), after <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> (1.9 billion tonnes) and <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (1.0 billion tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg/220px-Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg/330px-Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg/440px-Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_of_Kadavoor.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i> with small <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">wind-pollinated</a> flowers</div></div></div>
<p>Since sizable portions of sugarcane and maize crops are used for purposes other than human consumption, rice is the most important grain with regard to human nutrition and caloric intake, providing more than one-fifth of the <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">calories</a> consumed worldwide by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> There are many varieties of rice and culinary preferences tend to vary regionally.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_rice_cooked.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Brown_rice_cooked.JPG/220px-Brown_rice_cooked.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Brown_rice_cooked.JPG/330px-Brown_rice_cooked.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Brown_rice_cooked.JPG/440px-Brown_rice_cooked.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brown_rice_cooked.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cooked brown rice from Bhutan</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_grains_(IRRI).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg/220px-Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg/330px-Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg/440px-Rice_grains_%28IRRI%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_grains_(IRRI).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice can come in many shapes, colors and sizes.</div></div></div>
<p>Rice, a <a href="/wiki/Monocotyledon" title="Monocotyledon">monocot</a>, is normally grown as an <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual plant</a>, although in tropical areas it can survive as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a> and can produce a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ratoon" title="Ratoon">ratoon</a> crop for up to 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Rice cultivation is well-suited to countries and regions with low labor costs and high rainfall, as it is labor-intensive to cultivate and requires ample water. However, rice can be grown practically anywhere, even on a steep hill or mountain area with the use of water-controlling terrace systems. Although its parent species are native to Asia and certain parts of Africa, centuries of trade and exportation have made it commonplace in many cultures worldwide.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="452" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg/220px-Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg/330px-Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg/440px-Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oryza_sativa_-_K%C3%B6hler%E2%80%93s_Medizinal-Pflanzen-232.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa</a></i>, commonly known as Asian rice</div></div></div>
<p>The traditional method for cultivating rice is flooding the fields while, or after, setting the young seedlings. This simple method requires sound planning and servicing of the water damming and channeling, but reduces the growth of less robust weed and pest plants that have no submerged growth state, and deters <a href="/wiki/Vermin" title="Vermin">vermin</a>. While flooding is not mandatory for the cultivation of rice, all other methods of <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> require higher effort in <a href="/wiki/Weed_control" title="Weed control">weed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest control</a> during growth periods and a different approach for fertilizing the soil.
</p><p>The name <a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a> is usually used for species of the genera <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zizania_(genus)" title="Zizania (genus)">Zizania</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Porteresia" title="Porteresia">Porteresia</a></i>, both wild and domesticated, although the term may also be used for primitive or uncultivated varieties of <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza" title="Oryza">Oryza</a></i>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>First used in English in the middle of the 13th century, the word "rice" derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>ris</i>, which comes from <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italians</a> <i>riso</i>, in turn from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>oriza</i>, which derives from the Greek ὄρυζα (<i>oruza</i>). The <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word is the source of all European words (cf. Welsh <i>reis</i>, German <i>Reis</i>, Lithuanian <i>ryžiai</i>, Serbo-Croatian <i>riža</i>, Polish <i>ryż</i>, Dutch <i>rijst</i>, Hungarian <i>rizs</i>, Romanian <i>orez', Spanish </i>arroz<i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>The origin of the Greek word is unclear. It is sometimes held to be from the <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a> word (<i>arisi</i>), or rather Old Tamil <i>arici</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witzel_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-witzel-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Bhadriraju_Krishnamurti" title="Bhadriraju Krishnamurti">Krishnamurti</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> disagrees with the notion that Old Tamil <i>arici</i> is the source of the Greek term, and proposes that it was borrowed from descendants of <a href="/wiki/Proto-Dravidian_language" title="Proto-Dravidian language">Proto-Dravidian</a> *<i>wariñci</i> instead. Mayrhofer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWA_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWA-10">[10]</a></sup> suggests that the immediate source of the Greek word is to be sought in Old Iranian words of the types *<i>vrīz-</i> or *<i>vrinj-</i> (Source of the modern Persian word <i>Berenj</i>), but these are ultimately traced back to Indo-Aryan (as in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>vrīhí-</i>). <a href="/wiki/P._T._Srinivasa_Iyengar" title="P. T. Srinivasa Iyengar">P. T. Srinivasa Iyengar</a> assumed that the Sanskrit <i>vrīhí-</i> is derived from the Tamil <i>arici</i>, while <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Kittel" title="Ferdinand Kittel">Ferdinand Kittel</a> derived it from the Dravidian root <i>variki</i>. However, R. Swaminatha Aiyar believes that the Sanskrit <i>vrīhí-</i> is derived from a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Iranian_language" title="Proto-Indo-Iranian language">Proto-Indo-Iranian</a> root, and the Old Tamil <i>arici</i> is also of Indo-European origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<p>The rice plant can grow to 1–1.8 m (3.3–5.9 ft) tall, occasionally more depending on the variety and soil fertility. It has long, slender leaves 50–100 cm (20–39 in) long and 2–2.5 cm (0.79–0.98 in) broad. The small <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">wind-pollinated</a> flowers are produced in a branched arching to pendulous <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> 30–50 cm (12–20 in) long. The edible seed is a grain (<a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">caryopsis</a>) 5–12 mm (0.20–0.47 in) long and 2–3 mm (0.079–0.118 in) thick.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking">Cooking</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tteumul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="699" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tteumul.jpg/220px-Tteumul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tteumul.jpg/330px-Tteumul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Tteumul.jpg/440px-Tteumul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tteumul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bap_(food)" title="Bap (food)">tteumul</a>, water from the washing of rice</div></div></div>
<p>The varieties of rice are typically classified as long-, medium-, and short-grained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The grains of long-grain rice (high in <a href="/wiki/Amylose" title="Amylose">amylose</a>) tend to remain intact after cooking; medium-grain rice (high in <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>) becomes more sticky. Medium-grain rice is used for sweet dishes, for <i><a href="/wiki/Risotto" title="Risotto">risotto</a></i> in Italy, and many rice dishes, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Arr%C3%B2s_negre" title="Arròs negre">arròs negre</a></i>, in Spain. Some varieties of long-grain rice that are high in <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a>, known as Thai Sticky rice, are usually steamed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> A stickier medium-grain rice is used for <i><a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a></i>; the stickiness allows rice to hold its shape when molded. Medium-grain rice is used extensively in Japan, including to accompany savoury dishes, where it is usually served plain in a separate dish. Short-grain rice is often used for <a href="/wiki/Rice_pudding" title="Rice pudding">rice pudding</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Instant_rice" title="Instant rice">Instant rice</a> differs from <a href="/wiki/Parboiled_rice" title="Parboiled rice">parboiled rice</a> in that it is fully cooked and then dried, though there is a significant degradation in taste and texture. Rice flour and <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> often are used in <a href="/wiki/Batter_(cooking)" title="Batter (cooking)">batters</a> and breadings to increase crispiness.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">Preparation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="480" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg/220px-Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg/330px-Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg/440px-Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stages_of_rice_milling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unmilled to milled rice, from left to right, <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown rice</a>, rice with <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">germ</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white rice</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a>)</i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_Animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="1048" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rice_Animation.gif/220px-Rice_Animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rice_Animation.gif/330px-Rice_Animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rice_Animation.gif/440px-Rice_Animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_Animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A: Rice with <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a><br/>B: <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">Brown rice</a><br/>C: Rice with <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">germ</a><br/>D: <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">White rice</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a> <a href="/wiki/Crop_residue" title="Crop residue">residue</a><br/>E: Musenmai (Japanese: <a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%84%A1%E6%B4%97%E7%B1%B3" title="ja:無洗米">無洗米</a>), "Polished and ready to boil rice", literally, non-wash rice<br/>(1): <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">Chaff</a><br/>(2): <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">Bran</a><br/>(3): Bran <a href="/wiki/Crop_residue" title="Crop residue">residue</a><br/>(4): <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">Cereal germ</a><br/>(5): <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">Endosperm</a></div></div></div>
<p>Rice is typically rinsed before cooking to remove excess <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>. Rice produced in the US is usually fortified with vitamins and minerals, and rinsing will result in a loss of nutrients. Rice may be rinsed repeatedly until the rinse water is clear to improve the texture and taste.
</p><p>Rice may be soaked to decrease cooking time, conserve fuel, minimize exposure to high temperature, and reduce stickiness. For some <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a>, soaking improves the texture of the cooked rice by increasing expansion of the grains. Rice may be soaked for 30 minutes up to several hours.
</p><p>Brown rice may be soaked in warm water for 20 hours to stimulate <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a>. This process, called <a href="/wiki/Germinated_brown_rice" title="Germinated brown rice">germinated brown rice</a> (GBR),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> activates enzymes and enhances amino acids including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma-aminobutyric_acid" title="Gamma-aminobutyric acid">gamma-aminobutyric acid</a> to improve the nutritional value of brown rice. This method is a result of research carried out for the United Nations <a href="/wiki/International_Year_of_Rice" title="International Year of Rice">International Year of Rice</a>.
</p><p>Rice is cooked by <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">steaming</a>, and absorbs water during cooking. With the absorption method, rice may be cooked in a volume of water similar to the volume of rice. With the rapid-boil method, rice may be cooked in a large quantity of water which is drained before serving. Rapid-boil preparation is not desirable with enriched rice, as much of the enrichment additives are lost when the water is discarded. Electric <a href="/wiki/Rice_cooker" title="Rice cooker">rice cookers</a>, popular in Asia and Latin America, simplify the process of cooking rice. Rice (or any other grain) is sometimes quickly fried in oil or fat before boiling (for example <a href="/wiki/Saffron_rice" title="Saffron rice">saffron rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Risotto" title="Risotto">risotto</a>); this makes the cooked rice less sticky, and is a cooking style commonly called <a href="/wiki/Pilaf" title="Pilaf">pilaf</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>  or <a href="/wiki/Biryani" title="Biryani">biryani</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>  and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> .
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dishes">Dishes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_dishes" title="List of rice dishes">List of rice dishes</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Arab_cuisine" title="Arab cuisine">Arab cuisine</a>, rice is an ingredient of many soups and dishes with fish, poultry, and other types of meat. It is also used to stuff vegetables or is wrapped in grape leaves (<a href="/wiki/Dolma" title="Dolma">dolma</a>). When combined with milk, sugar, and honey, it is used to make desserts. In some regions, such as <a href="/wiki/Tabaristan" title="Tabaristan">Tabaristan</a>, bread is made using rice flour. Medieval Islamic texts spoke of medical uses for the plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Rice may also be made into <a href="/wiki/Congee" title="Congee">congee</a> (also called rice porridge or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_gruel" title="Rice gruel">rice gruel</a>) by adding more water than usual, so that the cooked rice is saturated with water, usually to the point that it disintegrates. Rice porridge is commonly eaten as a breakfast food, and is also a traditional food for the sick.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<p>Rice is the <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> of over half the world's population. It is the predominant dietary energy source for 17 countries in Asia and the Pacific, 9 countries in North and South America and 8 countries in Africa. Rice provides 20% of the world’s dietary energy supply, while wheat supplies 19% and maize (corn) 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cooked, unenriched, white, long-grained rice is composed of 68% water, 28% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>, 3% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table). In a 100 gram serving, it provides 130 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calories" title="Calories">calories</a> and contains no <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant amounts, with all less than 10% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_Value" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (DV) (table). Cooked, white, short-grained rice also provides 130 calories and contains moderate amounts of <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B vitamins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> (10–17% DV) per 100 gram amount (table).
</p><p>A detailed analysis of nutrient content of rice suggests that the nutrition value of rice varies based on a number of factors. It depends on the strain of rice, such as <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_rice" title="Red rice">red</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_rice" title="Black rice">black</a> (or purple) varieties having different prevalence across world regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-17">[17]</a></sup> It also depends on nutrient quality of the soil rice is grown in, whether and how the rice is polished or processed, the manner it is enriched, and how it is prepared before consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2018 <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) guideline showed that <a href="/wiki/Food_fortification" title="Food fortification">fortification</a> of rice to reduce <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> may involve different micronutrient strategies, including <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> only, iron with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">folic acid</a>, or iron with other <a href="/wiki/B_vitamins" title="B vitamins">B-complex vitamins</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thiamin" title="Thiamin">thiamin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">niacin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B6</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pantothenic_acid" title="Pantothenic acid">pantothenic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-17">[17]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> of <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> on the efficacy of rice fortification showed the strategy had the main effect of reducing the risk of <a href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency" title="Iron deficiency">iron deficiency</a> by 35% and increasing blood levels of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-17">[17]</a></sup> The guideline established a major recommendation: "Fortification of rice with iron is recommended as a public health strategy to improve the iron status of populations, in settings where rice is a staple food."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2018_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-who2018-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice grown experimentally under elevated <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> levels, similar to those predicted for the year 2100 <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">as a result of human activity</a>, had less iron, zinc, and protein, as well as lower levels of thiamin, <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a>, folic acid, and pantothenic acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arsenic_concerns">Arsenic concerns</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arsenic_toxicity" title="Arsenic toxicity">Arsenic toxicity</a></div>
<p>As arsenic is a natural element in soil, water, and air, the United States <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) monitors the levels of arsenic in foods, particularly in rice products used commonly for infant food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-23">[23]</a></sup> While growing, rice plants tend to absorb arsenic more readily than other food crops, requiring expanded testing by the FDA for possible arsenic-related risks associated with rice consumption in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-23">[23]</a></sup> In April 2016, the FDA proposed a limit of 100 parts per billion (ppb) for inorganic arsenic in infant <a href="/wiki/Rice_cereal" title="Rice cereal">rice cereal</a> and other foods to minimize exposure of infants to arsenic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-23">[23]</a></sup> For water contamination by arsenic, the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> has set a lower standard of 10 ppb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arsenic is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_1_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 1 carcinogens">Group 1 carcinogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-ars_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-fda-ars-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFSA2009_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA2009-25">[25]</a></sup> The amount of arsenic in rice varies widely with the greatest concentration in brown rice and rice grown on land formerly used to grow cotton, such as in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CR1112_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-CR1112-26">[26]</a></sup> White rice grown in Arkansas, Louisiana, Missouri, and Texas, which account collectively for 76 percent of American-produced rice, had higher levels of arsenic than other regions of the world studied, possibly because of past use of arsenic-based pesticides to control cotton weevils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Jasmine rice from Thailand and Basmati rice from Pakistan and India contain the least arsenic among rice varieties in one study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cpotera_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-cpotera-28">[28]</a></sup> China has set a limit of 150 ppb for arsenic in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacillus_cereus"><i>Bacillus cereus</i></span></h3>
<p>Cooked rice can contain <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_cereus" title="Bacillus cereus">Bacillus cereus</a></i> spores, which produce an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emetic" title="Emetic">emetic</a> toxin when left at 4–60 °C (39–140 °F). When storing cooked rice for use the next day, rapid cooling is advised to reduce the risk of toxin production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> One of the <a href="/wiki/Enterotoxin" title="Enterotoxin">enterotoxins</a> produced by <i>Bacillus cereus</i> is heat-resistant; reheating contaminated rice kills the bacteria, but does not destroy the toxin already present.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rice-growing_environments">Rice-growing environments</span></h2>
<p>Rice can be grown in different environments, depending upon water availability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Generally, rice does not thrive in a waterlogged area, yet it can survive and grow herein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and it can also survive flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><b>Lowland, rainfed</b>, which is drought prone, favors medium depth; waterlogged, submergence, and flood prone</li>
<li><b> Lowland, irrigated</b>, grown in both the wet season and the dry season</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_water_rice" title="Deep water rice">Deep water</a></b> or floating rice</li>
<li><b>Coastal wetland</b></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Upland_rice" title="Upland rice">Upland rice</a></b> is also known as <b>Ghaiya rice</b>, well known for its drought tolerance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_domestication_and_cultivation">History of domestication and cultivation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2912" data-file-width="4472" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg/220px-Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg/330px-Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg/440px-Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nihonbashi_bridge_in_Edo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice broker in 1820s Japan of the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> (<i>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/36_Views_of_Mount_Fuji" title="36 Views of Mount Fuji">36 Views of Mount Fuji</a>"</i> <a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Oryza_sativa#History_of_domestication_and_cultivation" title="Oryza sativa">Oryza sativa § History of domestication and cultivation</a></div>
<p>Wild rice, from which the crop was developed, may have its native range in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Chinese legends attribute the domestication of rice to <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a>, the legendary emperor of China and inventor of Chinese agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Genetic evidence has shown that rice originates from a single domestication 8,200–13,500 years ago<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-37">[37]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(China)" title="Pearl River (China)">Pearl River</a> valley region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">Ancient China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature1-38">[38]</a></sup> Previously, archaeological evidence had suggested that rice was domesticated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> valley region in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>From East Asia (particularly from China), rice was spread to Southeast and South Asia across the caravan routes of the central Asian steppes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature1_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature1-38">[38]</a></sup> Because Buddhist monks were vegetarian, they carried rice with them across the steppes. Thus, a Buddhist text called the Aggañña Sutta stated that "rice grows as long as Buddhism spreads."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Rice was introduced to Europe through Western Asia, and to the Americas through European colonization.
</p><p>There have been many debates on the origins of the domesticated rice. Genetic evidence published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America</a></i> (PNAS) shows that all forms of Asian rice, both <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i>, spring from a single domestication that occurred 8,200–13,500 years ago in China of the wild rice <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_rufipogon" title="Oryza rufipogon">Oryza rufipogon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas1_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1-37">[37]</a></sup> A 2012 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>, through a map of rice genome variation, indicated that the domestication of rice occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(China)" title="Pearl River (China)">Pearl River</a> valley region of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> based on the genetic evidence. From East Asia, rice was spread to South and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature1_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-nature1-38">[38]</a></sup>
Before this research, the commonly accepted view, based on archaeological evidence, is that rice was first domesticated in the region of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a> valley in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris-41">[41]</a></sup> Some scholars have also suggested that  it was first cultivated in the southern slopes of the Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Morphological studies of rice <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a> from the Diaotonghuan archaeological site clearly show the transition from the collection of wild rice to the cultivation of domesticated rice. The large number of wild rice phytoliths at the Diaotonghuan level dating from 12,000–11,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a> indicates that wild rice collection was part of the local means of subsistence. Changes in the morphology of Diaotonghuan phytoliths dating from 10,000–8,000 BP show that rice had by this time been domesticated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Soon afterwards the two major varieties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indica_rice" title="Indica rice">indica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japonica_rice" title="Japonica rice">japonica</a> rice were being grown in Central China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-harris-41">[41]</a></sup> In the late 3rd millennium BC, there was a rapid expansion of rice cultivation into mainland Southeast Asia and westwards across India and Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-harris-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2003, Korean archaeologists claimed to have discovered the world's oldest domesticated rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rice_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-rice-44">[44]</a></sup> Their 15,000-year-old age challenges the accepted view that rice cultivation originated in China about 12,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rice_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-rice-44">[44]</a></sup> These findings were received by academia with strong skepticism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and the results and their publicizing has been cited as being driven by a combination of nationalist and regional interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In 2011, a combined effort by the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_University_in_St._Louis" title="Washington University in St. Louis">Washington University in St. Louis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Purdue_University" title="Purdue University">Purdue University</a> has provided the strongest evidence yet that there is only one single origin of domesticated rice, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_Valley" title="Yangtze Valley">Yangtze Valley</a> of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice spread to the Middle East where, according to Zohary and Hopf (2000, p. 91), <i>O. sativa</i> was recovered from a grave at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> in Iran (dated to the 1st century AD).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_history">Regional history</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oryza_glaberrima" title="Oryza glaberrima">Oryza glaberrima</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambositra_04.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ambositra_04.jpg/220px-Ambositra_04.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ambositra_04.jpg/330px-Ambositra_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Ambositra_04.jpg/440px-Ambositra_04.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ambositra_04.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice crop in Madagascar</div></div></div>
<p>African rice has been cultivated for 3500 years. Between 1500 and 800 BC, <i><a href="/wiki/Oryza_glaberrima" title="Oryza glaberrima">Oryza glaberrima</a></i> propagated from its original centre, the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">delta</a>, and extended to Senegal. However, it never developed far from its original region. Its cultivation even declined in favour of the Asian species, which was introduced to East Africa early in the common era and spread westward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> African rice helped Africa conquer its famine of 1203.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Academies_Press_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Academies_Press-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SMB_Ricefields.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="958" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/SMB_Ricefields.jpg/220px-SMB_Ricefields.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/SMB_Ricefields.jpg/330px-SMB_Ricefields.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/SMB_Ricefields.jpg/440px-SMB_Ricefields.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SMB_Ricefields.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ricefields at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria,_Bulacan" title="Santa Maria, Bulacan">Santa Maria, Bulacan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dili_Reisfelder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="949" data-file-width="1429" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dili_Reisfelder.jpg/220px-Dili_Reisfelder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dili_Reisfelder.jpg/330px-Dili_Reisfelder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dili_Reisfelder.jpg/440px-Dili_Reisfelder.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dili_Reisfelder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice fields in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dili_District" title="Dili District">Dili</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg/220px-BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg/330px-BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg/440px-BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BeFunky_Chromatic_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indian women separating rice from straw</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg/220px-Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg/330px-Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg/440px-Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cambodian_farmers_planting_rice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cambodian women planting rice.</div></div></div>
<p>Today, the majority of all rice produced comes from China, India, Indonesia, Bangladesh, Vietnam, Thailand, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, Pakistan, Philippines, Korea and Japan. <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a> farmers still account for 87% of the world's total rice production.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nepal">Nepal</span></h4>
<p>Rice is the major food amongst all the ethnic groups in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Terai" title="Terai">Terai</a>, most rice varieties are cultivated during the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_of_South_Asia" title="Monsoon of South Asia">rainy season</a>. The principal rice growing season, known as "Berna-Bue Charne", is from June to July when water is sufficient for only a part of the fields; the subsidiary season, known as "Ropai, is from April to September, when there is usually enough water to sustain the cultivation of all rice fields. Farmers use irrigation channels throughout the cultivation seasons.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Banaue_Rice_Terraces" title="Banaue Rice Terraces">Banaue Rice Terraces</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Banaue" title="Banaue">Banaue</a> Rice Terraces (<a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">Filipino</a>: <i lang="fil">Hagdan-hagdang Palayan ng Banawe</i>) are 2,000-year-old <a href="/wiki/Terrace_(agriculture)" title="Terrace (agriculture)">terraces</a> that were carved into the mountains of <a href="/wiki/Ifugao" title="Ifugao">Ifugao</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> by ancestors of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igorot" title="Igorot">indigenous people</a>. The Rice Terraces are commonly referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Eighth_Wonder_of_the_World" title="Eighth Wonder of the World">Eighth Wonder of the World</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> It is commonly thought that the terraces were built with minimal equipment, largely by hand. The terraces are located approximately 1500 meters (5000 ft) above sea level. They are fed by an ancient <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> system from the rainforests above the terraces. It is said that if the steps were put end to end, it would encircle half the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tourism.gov.ph_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-tourism.gov.ph-54">[54]</a></sup>
The terraces are found in the province of Ifugao and the Ifugao people have been its caretakers. Ifugao culture revolves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-“Vietnam_&amp;_the_Philippines._Wonders_of_the_Asian_World_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-“Vietnam_&amp;_the_Philippines._Wonders_of_the_Asian_World-55">[55]</a></sup> around rice and the culture displays an elaborate array of celebrations linked with agricultural rites from rice cultivation to rice consumption. The harvest season generally calls for thanksgiving feasts, while the concluding harvest rites called <i>tango</i> or <i>tungul</i> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_rest" title="Day of rest">day of rest</a>) entails a strict taboo on any agricultural work. Partaking of the <i>bayah</i> (rice beer), rice cakes, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betel_nut" title="Betel nut">betel nut</a> constitutes an indelible practise during the festivities.
</p><p>The Ifugao people practice traditional farming spending most of their labor at their terraces and forest lands while occasionally tending to root crop cultivation. The Ifugaos have also<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tourism.gov.ph_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-tourism.gov.ph-54">[54]</a></sup> been known to culture edible shells, fruit trees, and other vegetables which have been exhibited among Ifugaos for generations. The building of the rice terraces consists of blanketing walls with stones and earth which are designed to draw water from a main irrigation canal above the terrace clusters. Indigenous rice terracing technologies have been identified with the Ifugao’s rice terraces such as their knowledge of water irrigation, stonework, earthwork and terrace maintenance. As their source of life and art, the rice terraces have sustained and shaped the lives of the community members.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</span></h4>
<p>Rice is the staple food amongst all the ethnic groups in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Sri_Lanka" title="Agriculture in Sri Lanka">Agriculture in Sri Lanka</a> mainly depends on the rice cultivation. Rice production is acutely dependent on rainfall and government supply necessity of water through irrigation channels throughout the cultivation seasons. The principal cultivation season, known as "Maha", is from October to March and the subsidiary cultivation season, known as "Yala", is from April to September. During Maha season, there is usually enough water to sustain the cultivation of all rice fields, nevertheless in Yala season there is only enough water for cultivation of half of the land extent.
</p><p>Traditional rice varieties are now making a comeback with the recent interest in green foods.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rice_production_in_Thailand" title="Rice production in Thailand">Rice production in Thailand</a></div>
<p>Rice is the main export of Thailand, especially white jasmine rice 105 (Dok Mali 105).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Thailand has a large number of rice varieties, 3,500 kinds with different characters, and five kinds of wild rice cultivates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In each region of the country there are different rice seed types. Their use depends on weather, atmosphere, and topography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ecology_of_life,_p._44,_1998_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_ecology_of_life,_p._44,_1998-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern region has both low lands and high lands. The farmers' usual crop is non-glutinous rice<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ecology_of_life,_p._44,_1998_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_ecology_of_life,_p._44,_1998-58">[58]</a></sup> such as Niew Sun Pah Tong rice. This rice is naturally protected from leaf disease, and its paddy (unmilled rice) (<a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>: <span lang="th">ข้าวเปลือก</span>) has a brown color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The northeastern region is a large area where farmers can cultivate about 36 million square meters of rice. Although most of it is plains and dry areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ecology_of_life,_p._45,_1998_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_ecology_of_life,_p._45,_1998-60">[60]</a></sup> white jasmine rice 105—the most famous Thai rice—can be grown there. White jasmine rice was developed in <a href="/wiki/Chonburi_Province" title="Chonburi Province">Chonburi Province</a> first and after that grown in many areas in the country, but the rice from this region has a high quality, because it's softer, whiter, and more fragrant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This rice can resist drought, acidic soil, and alkaline soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central region is mostly composed of plains. Most farmers grow Jao rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_ecology_of_life,_p._45,_1998_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_ecology_of_life,_p._45,_1998-60">[60]</a></sup> For example, Pathum Thani 1 rice which has qualities similar to white jasmine 105 rice. Its paddy has the color of thatch and the cooked rice has fragrant grains also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the southern region, most farmers transplant around boundaries to the flood plains or on the plains between mountains. Farming in the region is slower than other regions because the rainy season comes later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The popular rice varieties in this area are the Leb Nok Pattani seeds, a type of Jao rice. Its paddy has the color of thatch and it can be processed to make noodles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Companion_plant">Companion plant</span></h4>
<p>One of the earliest known examples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companion_plant" title="Companion plant">companion planting</a> is the growing of rice with <a href="/wiki/Azolla" title="Azolla">Azolla</a>, the mosquito fern, which covers the top of a fresh rice paddy's water, blocking out any competing plants, as well as fixing nitrogen from the atmosphere for the rice to use. The rice is planted when it is tall enough to poke out above the azolla. This method has been used for at least a thousand years.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span></h3>
<p>Rice was grown in some areas of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (southern Iraq). With the rise of Islam it moved north to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nisibin" title="Nisibin">Nisibin</a>, the southern shores of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilan_province" title="Gilan province">Gilan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazanderan" title="Mazanderan">Mazanderan</a> provinces of Iran)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pazuki_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-pazuki-66">[66]</a></sup> and then beyond the Muslim world into the valley of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga" title="Volga">Volga</a>. In Egypt, rice is mainly grown in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, rice came to be grown in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jordan_Valley_(Middle_East)" title="Jordan Valley (Middle East)">Jordan Valley</a>. Rice is also grown in Saudi Arabia at <a href="/wiki/Al-Hasa" title="Al-Hasa">Al-Hasa Oasis</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arr%C3%B2s,_sequer_(26462086444).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2273" data-file-width="1949" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Arr%C3%B2s%2C_sequer_%2826462086444%29.jpg/260px-Arr%C3%B2s%2C_sequer_%2826462086444%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Arr%C3%B2s%2C_sequer_%2826462086444%29.jpg/390px-Arr%C3%B2s%2C_sequer_%2826462086444%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Arr%C3%B2s%2C_sequer_%2826462086444%29.jpg/520px-Arr%C3%B2s%2C_sequer_%2826462086444%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arr%C3%B2s,_sequer_(26462086444).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice <a href="/wiki/Cropping_system" title="Cropping system">cropping</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Rice was known to the Classical world, being imported from Egypt, and perhaps west Asia. It was known to Greece (where it is still cultivated in Macedonia and Thrace) by returning soldiers from Alexander the Great's military expedition to Asia. Large deposits of rice from the first century AD have been found in Roman camps in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Moors</a> brought Asiatic rice to the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> in the 10th century. Records indicate it was grown in <a href="/wiki/Valencian_Community" title="Valencian Community">Valencia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majorca" title="Majorca">Majorca</a>. In Majorca, rice cultivation seems to have stopped after the Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconquesta" title="Reconquesta">conquest</a>, although historians are not certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims also brought rice to <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> with cultivation starting in the 9th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> where it was an important crop<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-67">[67]</a></sup> long before it is noted in the plain of <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> (1468) or in the Lombard plain (1475), where its cultivation was promoted by <a href="/wiki/Ludovico_Sforza" title="Ludovico Sforza">Ludovico Sforza</a>, Duke of Milan, and demonstrated in his model farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the 15th century, rice spread throughout Italy and then France, later propagating to all the continents during the age of European exploration.
</p><p>In European Russia, a short-grain, starchy rice similar to the Italian varieties, has been grown in the <a href="/wiki/Krasnodar_Krai" title="Krasnodar Krai">Krasnodar Krai</a>, and known in Russia as "Kuban Rice" or "Krasnodar Rice". In the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a> several <i>japonica</i> cultivars are grown in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primorye" title="Primorye">Primorye</a> around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khanka_lake" title="Khanka lake">Khanka lake</a>. Increasing scale of rice production in the region has recently brought criticism towards growers' alleged bad practices in regards to the environment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean_and_Latin_America">Caribbean and Latin America</span></h3>
<p>Most of the rice used today in the cuisine of the Americas is not native, but was introduced to Latin America and the Caribbean by <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European colonizers</a> at an early date. However, there are at least two native (endemic) species of rice present in the Amazon region of South America, and one or both were used by the indigenous inhabitants of the region to create the domesticated form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oryza_sp." title="Oryza sp.">Oryza sp.</a>, some 4000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish colonizers</a> introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_rice" title="Asian rice">Asian rice</a> to Mexico in the 1520s at <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> and their African slaves introduced it at about the same time to <a href="/wiki/State_of_Brazil" title="State of Brazil">colonial Brazil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slaveryinamerica.org_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-slaveryinamerica.org-72">[72]</a></sup> Recent scholarship suggests that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_slave_trade" title="African slave trade">enslaved Africans</a> played an active role in the establishment of rice in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> and that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_rice" title="African rice">African rice</a> was an important crop from an early period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Varieties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_and_bean" title="Rice and bean">rice and bean</a> dishes that were a staple dish along the peoples of West Africa remained a staple among their descendants subjected to <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Spanish_New_World_colonies" title="Slavery in the Spanish New World colonies">slavery in the Spanish New World colonies</a>, Brazil and elsewhere in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Academies_Press_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-National_Academies_Press-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_(0860)_(9684318485).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1664" data-file-width="2496" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_%280860%29_%289684318485%29.jpg/220px-FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_%280860%29_%289684318485%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_%280860%29_%289684318485%29.jpg/330px-FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_%280860%29_%289684318485%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_%280860%29_%289684318485%29.jpg/440px-FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_%280860%29_%289684318485%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FDA%27s_Rice_Tour-_Looking_for_Insights_on_Arsenic_(0860)_(9684318485).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> officials at a rice farm in California</div></div></div>
<p>In 1694, rice arrived in <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, probably originating from Madagascar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slaveryinamerica.org_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-slaveryinamerica.org-72">[72]</a></sup> Tradition (possibly apocryphal) has it that pirate <a href="/wiki/John_Thurber" title="John Thurber">John Thurber</a> was returning from a slave-trading voyage to Madagascar when he was blown off course and put into <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a> for repairs. While there he gave a bag of seed rice to explorer Dr. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Woodward_(colonist)" title="Henry Woodward (colonist)">Henry Woodward</a>, who planted the rice and experimented with it until finding that it grew exceptionally well in the wet Carolina soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">colonial</a> South Carolina and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> grew and amassed great wealth from the slave labor obtained from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Senegambia_(geography)" title="Senegambia (geography)">Senegambia</a> area of West Africa and from coastal Sierra Leone. At the port of Charleston, through which 40% of all American slave imports passed, slaves from this region of Africa brought the highest prices due to their prior knowledge of rice culture, which was put to use on the many rice <a href="/wiki/Plantations_in_the_American_South" title="Plantations in the American South">plantations</a> around <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_South_Carolina" title="Georgetown, South Carolina">Georgetown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia" title="Savannah, Georgia">Savannah</a>.
</p><p>From the enslaved Africans, plantation owners learned how to dyke the <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a> and periodically flood the fields. At first the rice was laboriously milled by hand using large mortars and pestles made of wood, then winnowed in <a href="/wiki/Muhlenbergia_filipes" title="Muhlenbergia filipes">sweetgrass</a> <a href="/wiki/Basket" title="Basket">baskets</a> (the making of which was another skill brought by slaves from Africa). The invention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_mill" title="Rice mill">rice mill</a> increased profitability of the crop, and the addition of water power for the mills in 1787 by <a href="/wiki/Millwright" title="Millwright">millwright</a> Jonathan Lucas was another step forward.
</p><p>Rice culture in the southeastern U.S. became less profitable with the loss of slave labor after the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, and it finally died out just after the turn of the 20th century. Today, people can visit the only remaining rice plantation in South Carolina that still has the original <a href="/wiki/Winnowing_barn" title="Winnowing barn">winnowing barn</a> and rice mill from the mid-19th century at the historic <a href="/wiki/Mansfield_Plantation" title="Mansfield Plantation">Mansfield Plantation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_South_Carolina" title="Georgetown, South Carolina">Georgetown, South Carolina</a>. The predominant strain of rice in the Carolinas was from Africa and was known as 'Carolina Gold'. The <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a> has been preserved and there are current attempts to reintroduce it as a commercially grown crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the southern United States, rice has been grown in southern <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, and east Texas since the mid-19th century. Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cajun" title="Cajun">Cajun</a> farmers grew rice in wet marshes and low-lying prairies where they could also farm <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a> when the fields were flooded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In recent years rice production has risen in North America, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_embayment" title="Mississippi embayment">Mississippi embayment</a> in the states of <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a> (see also <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_Delta" title="Arkansas Delta">Arkansas Delta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3216" data-file-width="4288" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg/250px-Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg/375px-Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg/500px-Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sacramento_rice_fields.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice paddy fields just north of the city of <a href="/wiki/Sacramento,_California" title="Sacramento, California">Sacramento, California</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Rice cultivation began in California during the <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a>, when an estimated 40,000 Chinese laborers immigrated to the state and grew small amounts of the grain for their own consumption. However, commercial production began only in 1912 in the town of <a href="/wiki/Richvale,_California" title="Richvale, California">Richvale</a> in <a href="/wiki/Butte_County,_California" title="Butte County, California">Butte County</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historic_Richvale_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Historic_Richvale-78">[78]</a></sup> By 2006, California produced the second-largest rice crop in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_region_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_region-79">[79]</a></sup> after Arkansas, with production concentrated in six counties north of <a href="/wiki/Sacramento,_California" title="Sacramento, California">Sacramento</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_economic_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_economic-80">[80]</a></sup> Unlike the Arkansas–Mississippi Delta region, California's production is dominated by short- and medium-grain <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice"><i>japonica</i></a> varieties, including cultivars developed for the local climate such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calrose" title="Calrose">Calrose</a>, which makes up as much as 85% of the state's crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_medium_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC_medium-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>References to "<a href="/wiki/Wild_rice" title="Wild rice">wild rice</a>" native to North America are to the unrelated <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zizania_palustris" title="Zizania palustris">Zizania palustris</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than 100 varieties of rice are commercially produced primarily in six states (Arkansas, Texas, Louisiana, Mississippi, Missouri, and California) in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-States_Department_of_Agriculture_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-States_Department_of_Agriculture-83">[83]</a></sup> According to estimates for the 2006 crop year, rice production in the U.S. is valued at $1.88 billion, approximately half of which is expected to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exported" title="Exported">exported</a>. The U.S. provides about 12% of world rice trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-States_Department_of_Agriculture_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-States_Department_of_Agriculture-83">[83]</a></sup> The majority of domestic utilization of U.S. rice is direct food use (58%), while 16% is used in each of processed foods and beer. 10% is found in pet food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-States_Department_of_Agriculture_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-States_Department_of_Agriculture-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span></h3>
<p>Rice was one of the earliest crops planted in Australia by British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Settlers" title="Settlers">settlers</a>, who had experience with rice plantations in the Americas and India.
</p><p>Although attempts to grow rice in the well-watered north of Australia have been made for many years, they have consistently failed because of inherent iron and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> toxicities in the soils and destruction by <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a>.
</p><p>In the 1920s, it was seen as a possible <a href="/wiki/Irrigation_in_Australia" title="Irrigation in Australia">irrigation</a> crop on soils within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murray-Darling_Basin" title="Murray-Darling Basin">Murray-Darling Basin</a> that were too heavy for the cultivation of fruit and too infertile for wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wadham,_Sir_Samuel_p._246_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wadham,_Sir_Samuel_p._246-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because irrigation water, despite the extremely low runoff of temperate Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> was (and remains) very cheap, the growing of rice was taken up by agricultural groups over the following decades. Californian varieties of rice were found suitable for the climate in the <a href="/wiki/Riverina" title="Riverina">Riverina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wadham,_Sir_Samuel_p._246_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wadham,_Sir_Samuel_p._246-84">[84]</a></sup> and the first mill opened at <a href="/wiki/Leeton,_New_South_Wales" title="Leeton, New South Wales">Leeton</a> in 1951.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="512" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg/220px-ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg/330px-ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg/440px-ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ABS-5439.0-InternationalMerchandiseImportsAustralia-StandardInternationalTradeClassificationCustomsValue-042Rice-A1828736L.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monthly value (<a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$ millions</a>) of rice imports to Australia since 1988</div></div></div>
<p>Even before this Australia's rice production greatly exceeded local needs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wadham,_Sir_Samuel_p._246_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wadham,_Sir_Samuel_p._246-84">[84]</a></sup> and rice exports to Japan have become a major source of foreign currency. Above-average rainfall from the 1950s to the middle 1990s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> encouraged the expansion of the Riverina rice industry, but its prodigious water use in a practically waterless region began to attract the attention of environmental scientists. These became severely concerned with declining flow in the <a href="/wiki/Snowy_River" title="Snowy River">Snowy River</a> and the lower <a href="/wiki/Murray_River" title="Murray River">Murray River</a>.
</p><p>Although rice growing in Australia is highly profitable due to the cheapness of land, several recent years of severe drought have led many to call for its elimination because of its effects on extremely fragile aquatic ecosystems. The Australian rice industry is somewhat opportunistic, with the area planted varying significantly from season to season depending on water allocations in the <a href="/wiki/Murray_River" title="Murray River">Murray</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murrumbidgee_River" title="Murrumbidgee River">Murrumbidgee</a> irrigation regions.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australian_Aboriginal" title="Australian Aboriginal">Australian Aboriginal</a> people have harvested native rice varieties for thousands of years, and there are ongoing efforts to grow commercial quantities of these species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>

<td colspan="2"><center><small>Source: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FAOSTAT" title="FAOSTAT">FAOSTAT</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-1">[1]</a></sup></small></center><small>
