<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RiceYield.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1600" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/RiceYield.png/220px-RiceYield.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/RiceYield.png/330px-RiceYield.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/RiceYield.png/440px-RiceYield.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RiceYield.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide rice production</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NP_India_burning_48_(6315309342).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="1280" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg/220px-NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg/330px-NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg/440px-NP_India_burning_48_%286315309342%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NP_India_burning_48_(6315309342).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burning of rice residues after harvest, to quickly prepare the land for <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> planting, around <a href="/wiki/Sangrur" title="Sangrur">Sangrur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab, India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 2014, world production of paddy rice was 741.5 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by China and India with a combined 49% of this total (table).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-1">[1]</a></sup> Other major producers were Indonesia, Bangladesh and Vietnam (table).
</p><p>The average world farm yield for rice in 2014 was 4.6 tonnes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-1">[1]</a></sup> Rice farms in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> were the most productive in 2014, with a nationwide average of 50.1 tonnes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faostat14_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-faostat14-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice is a major food staple and a mainstay for the rural population and their food security. It is mainly cultivated by small farmers in holdings of less than 1 hectare. Rice is also a wage commodity for workers in the cash crop or non-agricultural sectors. Rice is vital for the nutrition of much of the population in Asia, as well as in Latin America and the Caribbean and in Africa; it is central to the food security of over half the world population. Developing countries account for 95% of the total production, with China and India alone responsible for nearly half of the world output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAORice1_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAORice1-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many rice grain producing countries have significant losses post-harvest at the farm and because of poor roads, inadequate storage technologies, inefficient supply chains and farmer's inability to bring the produce into retail markets dominated by small shopkeepers. A World Bank – FAO study claims 8% to 26% of rice is lost in developing nations, on average, every year, because of post-harvest problems and poor infrastructure. Some sources claim the post-harvest losses to exceed 40%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAORice1_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAORice1-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Not only do these losses reduce food security in the world, the study claims that farmers in developing countries such as China, India and others lose approximately US$89 billion of income in preventable post-harvest farm losses, poor transport, the lack of proper storage and retail. One study claims that if these post-harvest grain losses could be eliminated with better infrastructure and retail network, in India alone enough food would be saved every year to feed 70 to 100 million people over a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="white_rice"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Processing">Processing</span></h3>
<p>The seeds of the rice plant are first milled using a <a href="/wiki/Rice_huller" title="Rice huller">rice huller</a> to remove the <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a> (the outer husks of the grain). At this point in the process, the product is called <a href="/wiki/Brown_rice" title="Brown rice">brown rice</a>. The milling may be continued, removing the <a href="/wiki/Bran" title="Bran">bran</a>, <i>i.e.</i>, the rest of the husk and the <a href="/wiki/Cereal_germ" title="Cereal germ">germ</a>, thereby creating <a href="/wiki/White_rice" title="White rice">white rice</a>. White rice, which keeps longer, lacks some important nutrients; moreover, in a limited diet which does not supplement the rice, brown rice helps to prevent the <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beriberi" title="Beriberi">beriberi</a>.
</p><p>Either by hand or in a <a href="/wiki/Rice_polisher" title="Rice polisher">rice polisher</a>, white rice may be buffed with glucose or <a href="/wiki/Talc" title="Talc">talc</a> powder (often called polished rice, though this term may also refer to white rice in general), <a href="/wiki/Parboiled_rice" title="Parboiled rice">parboiled</a>, or processed into flour. White rice may also be enriched by adding nutrients, especially those lost during the milling process. While the cheapest method of enriching involves adding a powdered blend of nutrients that will easily wash off (in the United States, rice which has been so treated requires a label warning against rinsing), more sophisticated methods apply nutrients directly to the grain, coating the grain with a water-insoluble substance which is resistant to washing.
</p><p>In some countries, a popular form, <a href="/wiki/Parboiled_rice" title="Parboiled rice">parboiled rice</a> (also known as <b>converted rice</b> and <b>easy-cook rice</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>) is subjected to a steaming or <a href="/wiki/Parboiling" title="Parboiling">parboiling</a> process while still a brown rice grain. The parboil process causes a gelatinisation of the starch in the grains. The grains become less brittle, and the color of the milled grain changes from white to yellow. The rice is then dried, and can then be milled as usual or used as brown rice. Milled parboiled rice is nutritionally superior to standard milled rice, because the process causes nutrients from the outer husk (especially <a href="/wiki/Thiamine" title="Thiamine">thiamine</a>) to move into the <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a>, so that less is subsequently lost when the husk is polished off during milling. Parboiled rice has an additional benefit in that it does not stick to the pan during cooking, as happens when cooking regular white rice. This type of rice is eaten in parts of India and countries of West Africa are also accustomed to consuming parboiled rice.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_bran" title="Rice bran">Rice bran</a>, called <i>nuka</i> in Japan, is a valuable commodity in Asia and is used for many daily needs. It is a moist, oily inner layer which is heated to produce oil. It is also used as a pickling bed in making <a href="/wiki/Nukazuke" title="Nukazuke">rice bran pickles</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Takuan" title="Takuan">takuan</a></i>.
</p><p>Raw rice may be ground into flour for many uses, including making many kinds of beverages, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Amazake" title="Amazake">amazake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horchata" title="Horchata">horchata</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Rice_milk" title="Rice milk">rice milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rice_wine" title="Rice wine">rice wine</a>. Rice does not contain <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>, so is suitable for people on a <a href="/wiki/Gluten-free_diet" title="Gluten-free diet">gluten-free diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PenaginiDilillo2013_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-PenaginiDilillo2013-93">[93]</a></sup> Rice may also be made into various types of <a href="/wiki/Noodle" title="Noodle">noodles</a>. Raw, wild, or brown rice may also be consumed by <a href="/wiki/Raw_foodism" title="Raw foodism">raw-foodist</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fruitarians" title="Fruitarians">fruitarians</a> if soaked and <a href="/wiki/Sprouting" title="Sprouting">sprouted</a> (usually a week to 30 days – gaba rice).
</p><p>Processed rice seeds must be boiled or steamed before eating. Boiled rice may be further fried in <a href="/wiki/Cooking_oil" title="Cooking oil">cooking oil</a> or butter (known as <a href="/wiki/Fried_rice" title="Fried rice">fried rice</a>), or beaten in a tub to make <i><a href="/wiki/Mochi" title="Mochi">mochi</a></i>.
</p><p>Rice is a good source of protein and a staple food in many parts of the world, but it is not a <a href="/wiki/Complete_protein" title="Complete protein">complete protein</a>: it does not contain all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acids" title="Essential amino acids">essential amino acids</a> in sufficient amounts for good health, and should be combined with other sources of protein, such as nuts, seeds, beans, fish, or meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protein_content_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Protein_content-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice, like other cereal <a href="/wiki/Caryopsis" title="Caryopsis">grains</a>, can be <a href="/wiki/Puffed_rice" title="Puffed rice">puffed (or popped)</a>. This process takes advantage of the grains' water content and typically involves heating grains in a special chamber. Further puffing is sometimes accomplished by processing puffed pellets in a low-<a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> chamber. The <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a> means either lowering the local pressure or raising the water temperature results in an increase in <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> prior to water <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, resulting in a puffy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Texture_(food)" title="Texture (food)">texture</a>. Bulk raw rice density is about 0.9 g/cm³. It decreases to less than one-tenth that when puffed.
</p>
<h3><span id="Harvesting.2C_drying_and_milling"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Harvesting,_drying_and_milling">Harvesting, drying and milling</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice-combine-harvester,_Katori-city,_Japan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg/220px-Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg/330px-Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg/440px-Rice-combine-harvester%2C_Katori-city%2C_Japan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice-combine-harvester,_Katori-city,_Japan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice combine harvester <a href="/wiki/Katori,_Chiba" title="Katori, Chiba">Katori-city</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba Prefecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1316" data-file-width="2560" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/220px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/330px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg/440px-Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_farmers_Mae_Wang_Chiang_Mai_Province.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the harvest, rice straw is gathered in the traditional way from small paddy fields in <a href="/wiki/Mae_Wang_District" title="Mae Wang District">Mae Wang District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Mai_Province" title="Chiang Mai Province">Chiang Mai Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a></div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">Paddy field</a></div>
<p>Unmilled rice, known as "paddy" (Indonesia and Malaysia: padi; Philippines, palay), is usually harvested when the grains have a moisture content of around 25%. In most Asian countries, where rice is almost entirely the product of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smallholder" title="Smallholder">smallholder</a> agriculture, harvesting is carried out manually, although there is a growing interest in mechanical harvesting. Harvesting can be carried out by the farmers themselves, but is also frequently done by seasonal labor groups. Harvesting is followed by <a href="/wiki/Threshing" title="Threshing">threshing</a>, either immediately or within a day or two. Again, much threshing is still carried out by hand but there is an increasing use of mechanical threshers. Subsequently, paddy needs to be dried to bring down the moisture content to no more than 20% for milling.
</p><p>A familiar sight in several Asian countries is paddy laid out to dry along roads. However, in most countries the bulk of drying of marketed paddy takes place in mills, with village-level drying being used for paddy to be consumed by farm families. Mills either sun dry or use mechanical driers or both. Drying has to be carried out quickly to avoid the formation of molds. Mills range from simple <a href="/wiki/Rice_huller" title="Rice huller">hullers</a>, with a throughput of a couple of tonnes a day, that simply remove the outer husk, to enormous operations that can process 4,000 tonnes a day and produce highly polished rice. A good mill can achieve a paddy-to-rice conversion rate of up to 72% but smaller, inefficient mills often struggle to achieve 60%. These smaller mills often do not buy paddy and sell rice but only service farmers who want to mill their paddy for their own consumption.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h3>
<p>Because of the importance of rice to human nutrition and food security in Asia, the domestic rice markets tend to be subject to considerable state involvement. While the private sector plays a leading role in most countries, agencies such as <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Bureau_of_Logistics" title="Indonesian Bureau of Logistics">BULOG</a> in Indonesia, the <a href="/wiki/National_Food_Authority_(Philippines)" title="National Food Authority (Philippines)">NFA</a> in the Philippines, VINAFOOD in Vietnam and the <a href="/wiki/Food_Corporation_of_India" title="Food Corporation of India">Food Corporation of India</a> are all heavily involved in purchasing of paddy from farmers or rice from mills and in distributing rice to poorer people. BULOG and NFA monopolise rice imports into their countries while VINAFOOD controls all exports from Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nellu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="997" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Nellu.JPG/220px-Nellu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Nellu.JPG/330px-Nellu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Nellu.JPG/440px-Nellu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nellu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drying rice in <a href="/wiki/Peravoor" title="Peravoor">Peravoor</a>, India</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h3>
<p>World trade figures are very different from those for production, as less than 8% of rice produced is traded internationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-96">[96]</a></sup> In economic terms, the global rice trade was a small fraction of 1% of world mercantile trade. Many countries consider rice as a strategic food staple, and various governments subject its trade to a wide range of controls and interventions.
</p><p>Developing countries are the main players in the world rice trade, accounting for 83% of exports and 85% of imports. While there are numerous importers of rice, the exporters of rice are limited. Just five countries – Thailand, Vietnam, China, the United States and India – in decreasing order of exported quantities, accounted for about three-quarters of world rice exports in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAORice1_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FAORice1-89">[89]</a></sup> However, this ranking has been rapidly changing in recent years. In 2010, the three largest exporters of rice, in decreasing order of quantity exported were Thailand, Vietnam and India. By 2012, India became the largest exporter of rice with a 100% increase in its exports on year-to-year basis, and Thailand slipped to third position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Together, Thailand, Vietnam and India accounted for nearly 70% of the world rice exports.
</p><p>The primary variety exported by Thailand and Vietnam were <a href="/wiki/Jasmine_rice" title="Jasmine rice">Jasmine rice</a>, while exports from India included aromatic <a href="/wiki/Basmati" title="Basmati">Basmati</a> variety. China, an exporter of rice in early 2000s, was a net importer of rice in 2010 and will become the largest net importer, surpassing Nigeria, in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> According to a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> report, the world's largest exporters of rice in 2012 were India (9.75 million tonnes), Vietnam (7 million tonnes), Thailand (6.5 million tonnes), Pakistan (3.75 million tonnes) and the United States (3.5 million tonnes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Major importers usually include Nigeria, Indonesia, Bangladesh, Saudi Arabia, Iran, Iraq, Malaysia, the Philippines, Brazil and some African and <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> countries. In common with other West African countries, Nigeria is actively promoting domestic production. However, its very heavy import duties (110%) open it to smuggling from neighboring countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Parboiled rice is particularly popular in Nigeria. Although China and India are the two largest producers of rice in the world, both countries consume the majority of the rice produced domestically, leaving little to be traded internationally.
</p>
<h3><span id="World.27s_most_productive_rice_farms_and_farmers"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World's_most_productive_rice_farms_and_farmers">World's most productive rice farms and farmers</span></h3>
<p>The average world yield for rice was 4.3 tonnes per hectare, in 2010.
</p><p>Australian rice farms were the most productive in 2010, with a nationwide average of 10.8 tonnes per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Yuan Longping of China National Hybrid Rice Research and Development Center, China, set a world record for rice yield in 2010 at 19 tonnes per hectare on a demonstration plot. In 2011, this record was surpassed by an Indian farmer, Sumant Kumar, with 22.4 tonnes per hectare in Bihar. Both these farmers claim to have employed newly developed rice breeds and <a href="/wiki/System_of_Rice_Intensification" title="System of Rice Intensification">System of Rice Intensification</a> (SRI), a recent innovation in rice farming. SRI is claimed to have set new national records in rice yields, within the last 10 years, in many countries. The claimed Chinese and Indian yields have yet to be demonstrated on seven-hectare lots and to be reproducible over two consecutive years on the same farm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Price">Price</span></h2>
<p>In late 2007 to May 2008, the price of grains rose greatly due to droughts in major producing countries (particularly Australia), increased use of grains for animal feed and US subsidies for bio-fuel production. Although there was no shortage of rice on world markets this general upward trend in grain prices led to panic buying by consumers, government rice export bans (in particular, by Vietnam and India) and inflated import orders by the Philippines marketing board, the National Food Authority. This caused significant rises in rice prices. In late April 2008, prices hit 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_cent" title="US cent">US cents</a> a <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>, twice the price of seven months earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cyclone_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-cyclone-107">[107]</a></sup> Over the period of 2007 to 2013, the Chinese government has substantially increased the price it pays domestic farmers for their rice, rising to <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>500 per metric ton by 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-96">[96]</a></sup> The 2013 price of rice originating from other southeast Asian countries was a comparably low <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>350 per metric ton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cendrowski2013_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-cendrowski2013-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 30, 2008, Thailand announced plans for the creation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Rice_Exporting_Countries" title="Organisation of Rice Exporting Countries">Organisation of Rice Exporting Countries</a> (OREC) with the intention that this should develop into a price-fixing <a href="/wiki/Cartel" title="Cartel">cartel</a> for rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> However, little progress had been made by mid-2011 to achieve this.
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As of 2009 world food consumption of rice was 531.6 million metric tons of paddy equivalent (354,603 of milled equivalent), while the far largest consumers were China consuming 156.3 million metric tons of paddy equivalent (29.4% of the world consumption) and India consuming 123.5 million metric tons of paddy equivalent (23.3% of the world consumption).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-con_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-con-110">[110]</a></sup> Between 1961 and 2002, per capita consumption of rice increased by 40%.
</p><p>Rice is the most important crop in Asia. In Cambodia, for example, 90% of the total agricultural area is used for rice production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>U.S. rice consumption has risen sharply over the past 25 years, fueled in part by commercial applications such as beer production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Almost one in five adult Americans now report eating at least half a serving of white or brown rice per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impacts">Environmental impacts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NP_Rice_Emissions18_(5687953086).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg/220px-NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg/330px-NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg/440px-NP_Rice_Emissions18_%285687953086%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NP_Rice_Emissions18_(5687953086).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Work by the <a href="/wiki/International_Center_for_Tropical_Agriculture" title="International Center for Tropical Agriculture">International Center for Tropical Agriculture</a> to measure the greenhouse gas emissions of rice production.</div></div></div>
<p>Rice cultivation on wetland rice fields is thought to be responsible for 11% of the anthropogenic methane emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Rice requires slightly more water to produce than other grains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Rice production uses almost a third of Earth’s fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Long-term flooding of rice fields cuts the soil off from atmospheric oxygen and causes anaerobic fermentation of organic matter in the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heinz_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-heinz-117">[117]</a></sup> Methane production from rice cultivation contributes ~1.5% of anthropogenic greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> is twenty times more potent a greenhouse gas than <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2010 study found that, as a result of rising temperatures and decreasing solar radiation during the later years of the 20th century, the rice yield growth rate has decreased in many parts of Asia, compared to what would have been observed had the temperature and solar radiation trends not occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The yield growth rate had fallen 10–20% at some locations. The study was based on records from 227 farms in Thailand, Vietnam, Nepal, India, China, Bangladesh, and Pakistan. The mechanism of this falling yield was not clear, but might involve increased respiration during warm nights, which expends energy without being able to photosynthesize.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rainfall">Rainfall</span></h3>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span></h3>
<p>Rice requires high temperature above 20 °C (68 °F) but not more than 35 to 40 °C (95 to 104 °F). Optimum temperature is around 30 °C (T<sub>max</sub>) and 20 °C (T<sub>min</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_radiation">Solar radiation</span></h3>
<p>The amount of solar radiation received during the 45 days leading up to harvest determines final crop output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-aaa-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_water_vapor">Atmospheric water vapor</span></h3>
<p>High water vapor content (in humid tropics) subjects unusual stress which favors the spread of fungal and bacterial diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-aaa-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind">Wind</span></h3>
<p>Light wind transports CO<sub>2</sub> to the leaf canopy but strong wind causes severe damage and may lead to sterility (due to pollen dehydration, spikelet sterility, and abortive endosperms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-aaa-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests_and_diseases">Pests and diseases</span></h2>
<p>Rice pests are any <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a> with the potential to reduce the yield or value of the rice crop (or of rice seeds).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Rice pests include weeds, <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a>, insects, nematode, rodents, and birds. A variety of factors can contribute to pest outbreaks, including climatic factors, improper irrigation, the overuse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecticides" title="Insecticides">insecticides</a> and high rates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fertilizer" title="Nitrogen fertilizer">nitrogen fertilizer</a> application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Weather conditions also contribute to pest outbreaks. For example, rice <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gall_midge" title="Gall midge">gall midge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spodoptera_mauritia" title="Spodoptera mauritia">army worm</a> outbreaks tend to follow periods of high rainfall early in the wet season, while <a href="/wiki/Thrips" title="Thrips">thrips</a> outbreaks are associated with drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_rice_grasshopper_(Oxya_chinensis).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="3432" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg/220px-Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg/330px-Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg/440px-Chinese_rice_grasshopper_%28Oxya_chinensis%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_rice_grasshopper_(Oxya_chinensis).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese rice grasshopper<br/>(<i>Oxya chinensis</i>)<br/><a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Major rice insect pests include: the <a href="/wiki/Brown_planthopper" title="Brown planthopper">brown planthopper</a> (BPH),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> several spp. of <a href="/wiki/Stemborer" title="Stemborer">stemborers</a> – including those in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Scirpophaga" title="Scirpophaga">Scirpophaga</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chilo_suppressalis" title="Chilo suppressalis">Chilo</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKB_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-RKB-127">[127]</a></sup> the rice <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gall_midge" title="Gall midge">gall midge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> several spp. of rice bugs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j1_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-j1-129">[129]</a></sup> – notably in the genus <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leptocorisa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leptocorisa (page does not exist)">Leptocorisa</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Cnaphalocrocis_medinalis" title="Cnaphalocrocis medinalis">rice leafroller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice_weevil" title="Rice weevil">rice weevils</a> and the Chinese rice grasshopper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fall_armyworm" title="Fall armyworm">fall army worm</a>, a species of Lepidoptera, also targets and causes damage to rice crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_diseases" title="List of rice diseases">List of rice diseases</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_blast" title="Rice blast">Rice blast</a>, caused by the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Magnaporthe_grisea" title="Magnaporthe grisea">Magnaporthe grisea</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> is the most significant disease affecting rice cultivation. Other major rice diseases include: <a href="/wiki/Rhizoctonia_solani" title="Rhizoctonia solani">sheath blight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice_ragged_stunt_virus" title="Rice ragged stunt virus">rice ragged stunt</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector</a>: BPH), and tungro (vector: <i>Nephotettix</i> spp).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> There is also an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascomycete" title="Ascomycete">ascomycete</a> fungus, <i><a href="/wiki/Cochliobolus_miyabeanus" title="Cochliobolus miyabeanus">Cochliobolus miyabeanus</a></i>, that causes brown spot disease in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nematodes">Nematodes</span></h3>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> species infect rice crops, causing diseases such as Ufra (Ditylenchus dipsaci), White tip disease (Aphelenchoide bessei), and root knot disease (Meloidogyne graminicola). Some nematode species such as <i>Pratylenchus</i> spp. are most dangerous in upland rice of all parts of the world. Rice root nematode (<i><a href="/wiki/Hirschmanniella_oryzae" title="Hirschmanniella oryzae">Hirschmanniella oryzae</a></i>) is a migratory endoparasite which on higher inoculum levels will lead to complete destruction of a rice crop. Beyond being obligate parasites, they also decrease the vigor of plants and increase the plants' susceptibility to other pests and diseases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_pests">Other pests</span></h3>
<p>These include the apple snail <i><a href="/wiki/Pomacea_canaliculata" title="Pomacea canaliculata">Pomacea canaliculata</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panicle_rice_mite" title="Panicle rice mite">panicle rice mite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> and the weed <i><a href="/wiki/Echinochloa" title="Echinochloa">Echinochloa</a> crusgali</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated_pest_management">Integrated pest management</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">Integrated pest management</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Crop_protection" title="Crop protection">Crop protection</a> scientists are trying to develop rice pest management techniques which are <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable</a>. In other words, to manage crop pests in such a manner that future crop production is not threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Sustainable pest management is based on four principles: biodiversity, host plant resistance (HPR), landscape ecology, and hierarchies in a landscape – from biological to social.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> At present, rice pest management includes cultural techniques, pest-resistant rice varieties, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> (which include <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticide</a>). Increasingly, there is evidence that farmers' pesticide applications are often unnecessary, and even facilitate pest outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> By reducing the populations of natural enemies of rice pests,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> misuse of insecticides can actually lead to pest outbreaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> (IRRI) demonstrated in 1993 that an 87.5% reduction in pesticide use can lead to an overall drop in pest numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> IRRI also conducted two campaigns in 1994 and 2003, respectively, which discouraged insecticide misuse and smarter pest management in Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice plants produce their own chemical defenses to protect themselves from pest attacks. Some synthetic chemicals, such as the herbicide 2,4-D, cause the plant to increase the production of certain defensive chemicals and thereby increase the plant’s resistance to some types of pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Conversely, other chemicals, such as the insecticide <a href="/wiki/Imidacloprid" title="Imidacloprid">imidacloprid</a>, can induce changes in the gene expression of the rice that cause the plant to become more susceptible to attacks by certain types of pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> 5-<a href="/wiki/Alkylresorcinol" title="Alkylresorcinol">Alkylresorcinols</a> are chemicals that can also be found in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Botanicals, so-called "natural pesticides", are used by some farmers in an attempt to control rice pests. Botanicals include extracts of leaves, or a mulch of the leaves themselves. Some upland rice farmers in Cambodia spread chopped leaves of the bitter bush (<i><a href="/wiki/Chromolaena_odorata" title="Chromolaena odorata">Chromolaena odorata</a></i>) over the surface of fields after planting. This practice probably helps the soil retain moisture and thereby facilitates seed germination. Farmers also claim the leaves are a natural fertilizer and helps suppress weed and insect infestations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/220px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/330px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG/440px-Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chloroxylon_from_India.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chloroxylon is used for Pest Management in Organic Rice Cultivation in Chhattisgarh, India</div></div></div>
<p>Among rice cultivars, there are differences in the responses to, and recovery from, pest damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j1_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-j1-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Many rice varieties have been selected for resistance to insect pests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Therefore, particular cultivars are recommended for areas prone to certain pest problems. The genetically based ability of a rice variety to withstand pest attacks is called resistance. Three main types of plant resistance to pests are recognized as nonpreference, antibiosis, and tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j2_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-j2-157">[157]</a></sup> Nonpreference (or antixenosis) describes host plants which insects prefer to avoid; antibiosis is where insect survival is reduced after the ingestion of host tissue; and tolerance is the capacity of a plant to produce high yield or retain high quality despite <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_stored_product_entomology" title="Home stored product entomology">insect infestation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over time, the use of pest-resistant rice varieties selects for pests that are able to overcome these mechanisms of resistance. When a rice variety is no longer able to resist pest infestations, resistance is said to have broken down. Rice varieties that can be widely grown for many years in the presence of pests and retain their ability to withstand the pests are said to have durable resistance. Mutants of popular rice varieties are regularly screened by plant breeders to discover new sources of durable resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-j2_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-j2-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasitic_weeds">Parasitic weeds</span></h3>
<p>Rice is parasitized by the weed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eudicot" title="Eudicot">eudicot</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Striga_hermonthica" title="Striga hermonthica">Striga hermonthica</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> which is of local importance for this crop.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecotypes_and_cultivars">Ecotypes and cultivars</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rice_varieties" title="List of rice varieties">List of rice varieties</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_diversity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="2400" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Rice_diversity.jpg/220px-Rice_diversity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Rice_diversity.jpg/330px-Rice_diversity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Rice_diversity.jpg/440px-Rice_diversity.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_diversity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rice seed collection from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IRRI" title="IRRI">IRRI</a></div></div></div>
<p>While most rice is bred for crop quality and productivity, there are varieties selected for characteristics such as texture, smell, and firmness. There are four major categories of rice worldwide: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indica_rice" title="Indica rice">indica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japonica_rice" title="Japonica rice">japonica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aromatic_rice" title="Aromatic rice">aromatic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutinous_rice" title="Glutinous rice">glutinous</a>. The different varieties of rice are not considered interchangeable, either in food preparation or agriculture, so as a result, each major variety is a completely separate market from other varieties. It is common for one variety of rice to rise in price while another one drops in price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice cultivars also fall into groups according to environmental conditions, season of planting, and season of harvest, called ecotypes. Some major groups are the Japan-type (grown in Japan), "buly" and "tjereh" types (Indonesia); "aman" (main winter crop), "aus" ("aush", summer), and "boro" (spring) (Bengal and Assam).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banglapedia_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banglapedia-163">[163]</a></sup> Cultivars exist that are adapted to deep flooding, and these are generally called "floating rice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest collection of rice cultivars is at the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> in the Philippines, with over 100,000 rice accessions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> held in the International Rice Genebank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Rice <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> are often classified by their grain shapes and texture. For example, Thai <a href="/wiki/Jasmine_rice" title="Jasmine rice">Jasmine rice</a> is long-grain and relatively less sticky, as some long-grain rice contains less <a href="/wiki/Amylopectin" title="Amylopectin">amylopectin</a> than short-grain cultivars. Chinese restaurants often serve long-grain as plain unseasoned steamed rice though short-grain rice is common as well. Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mochigome" title="Mochigome">mochi rice</a> and Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sticky_rice" title="Sticky rice">sticky rice</a> are short-grain. Chinese people use sticky rice which is properly known as "glutinous rice" (note: glutinous refer to the glue-like characteristic of rice; does not refer to "gluten") to make <a href="/wiki/Zongzi" title="Zongzi">zongzi</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese table rice</a> is a sticky, short-grain rice. Japanese <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a> rice is another kind as well.
</p><p>Indian rice cultivars include long-grained and aromatic <a href="/wiki/Basmati" title="Basmati">Basmati</a> (ਬਾਸਮਤੀ) (grown in the North), long and medium-grained <a href="/wiki/Patna_rice" title="Patna rice">Patna rice</a>, and in South India (<a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>) short-grained <a href="/wiki/Sona_Masuri" title="Sona Masuri">Sona Masuri</a> (also called as Bangaru theegalu). In the state of Tamil Nadu, the most prized cultivar is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponni_Rice" title="Ponni Rice"><i>ponni</i></a> which is primarily grown in the delta regions of the <a href="/wiki/Kaveri" title="Kaveri">Kaveri</a> River. <a href="/wiki/Kaveri" title="Kaveri">Kaveri</a> is also referred to as ponni in the South and the name reflects the geographic region where it is grown. In the Western Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>, a short grain variety called <a href="/wiki/Ambemohar" title="Ambemohar">Ambemohar</a> is very popular. This rice has a characteristic fragrance of Mango blossom.
</p><p>Aromatic rices have definite aromas and flavors; the most noted cultivars are Thai fragrant rice, Basmati, Patna rice, Vietnamese fragrant rice, and a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> cultivar from America, sold under the trade name Texmati. Both Basmati and Texmati have a mild <a href="/wiki/Popcorn" title="Popcorn">popcorn</a>-like aroma and flavor. In Indonesia, there are also <i>red</i> and <i>black</i> cultivars.
</p><p>High-yield cultivars of rice suitable for cultivation in Africa and other dry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>, called the <a href="/wiki/New_Rice_for_Africa" title="New Rice for Africa">new rice for Africa</a> (NERICA) cultivars, have been developed. It is hoped that their cultivation will improve <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> in West Africa.
</p><p>Draft <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genomes" title="Genomes">genomes</a> for the two most common rice cultivars, <i>indica</i> and <i>japonica</i>, were published in April 2002. Rice was chosen as a <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for the biology of grasses because of its relatively small genome (~430 mega<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_pairs" title="Base pairs">base pairs</a>). Rice was the first crop with a complete genome sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 16, 2002, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> declared the year 2004 the International Year of Rice. The declaration was sponsored by more than 40 countries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High-yielding_varieties">High-yielding varieties</span></h3>
<p>The high-yielding varieties are a group of crops created intentionally during the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> to increase global food production. This project enabled labor markets in Asia to shift away from agriculture, and into industrial sectors. The first "Rice Car", IR8 was produced in 1966 at the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> which is based in the Philippines at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Philippines" title="University of the Philippines">University of the Philippines</a>' Los Baños site. IR8 was created through a cross between an Indonesian variety named "Peta" and a Chinese variety named "Dee Geo Woo Gen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ir8_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-ir8-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientists have identified and cloned many genes involved in the <a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">gibberellin</a> signaling pathway, including GAI1 (<a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">Gibberellin</a> Insensitive) and SLR1 (Slender Rice).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Disruption of <a href="/wiki/Gibberellin" title="Gibberellin">gibberellin</a> signaling can lead to significantly reduced stem growth leading to a dwarf phenotype. Photosynthetic investment in the stem is reduced dramatically as the shorter plants are inherently more stable mechanically. Assimilates become redirected to grain production, amplifying in particular the effect of chemical fertilizers on commercial yield. In the presence of nitrogen fertilizers, and intensive crop management, these varieties increase their yield two to three times.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_potential">Future potential</span></h3>
<p>As the UN Millennium Development project seeks to spread global economic development to Africa, the "Green Revolution" is cited as the model for economic development. With the intent of replicating the successful Asian boom in agronomic productivity, groups like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_Institute" title="Earth Institute">Earth Institute</a> are doing research on African agricultural systems, hoping to increase productivity. An important way this can happen is the production of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Rices_for_Africa" title="New Rices for Africa">New Rices for Africa</a>" (NERICA). These rices, selected to tolerate the low input and harsh growing conditions of African agriculture, are produced by the African Rice Center, and billed as technology "from Africa, for Africa". The NERICA have appeared in <i>The New York Times</i> (October 10, 2007) and <i>International Herald Tribune</i> (October 9, 2007), trumpeted as miracle crops that will dramatically increase rice yield in Africa and enable an economic resurgence. Ongoing research in China to develop <a href="/wiki/Perennial_rice" title="Perennial rice">perennial rice</a> could result in enhanced sustainability and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_rice">Golden rice</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">Golden rice</a></div>
<p>Rice kernels do not contain <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a>, so people who obtain most of their calories from rice are at risk of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A_deficiency" title="Vitamin A deficiency">vitamin A deficiency</a>. German and Swiss researchers have <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetically engineered</a> rice to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-carotene" title="Beta-carotene">beta-carotene</a>, the precursor to vitamin A, in the rice kernel. The beta-carotene turns the processed (white) rice a "gold" color, hence the name "golden rice." The beta-carotene is converted to vitamin A in humans who consume the rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Although some rice strains produce beta-carotene in the hull, no non-genetically engineered strains have been found that produce beta-carotene in the kernel, despite the testing of thousands of strains. Additional efforts are being made to improve the quantity and quality of other nutrients in golden rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcgh_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-gcgh-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> is currently further developing and evaluating Golden Rice as a potential new way to help address vitamin A deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expression_of_human_proteins">Expression of human proteins</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ventria_Bioscience" title="Ventria Bioscience">Ventria Bioscience</a> has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" title="Genetically modified">genetically modified</a> rice to <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">express</a> <a href="/wiki/Lactoferrin" title="Lactoferrin">lactoferrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysozyme" title="Lysozyme">lysozyme</a> which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a> usually found in <a href="/wiki/Breast_milk" title="Breast milk">breast milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_serum_albumin" title="Human serum albumin">human serum albumin</a>, These proteins have <a href="/wiki/Antiviral_protein" title="Antiviral protein">antiviral</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibacterial" title="Antibacterial">antibacterial</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antifungal_protein" title="Antifungal protein">antifungal</a> effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rice containing these added proteins can be used as a component in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_solution" title="Oral rehydration solution">oral rehydration solutions</a> which are used to treat <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrheal</a> diseases, thereby shortening their duration and reducing recurrence. Such supplements may also help reverse <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood-tolerant_rice">Flood-tolerant rice</span></h3>
<p>Due to the varying levels that water can reach in regions of cultivation, <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_rice" title="Deepwater rice">flood tolerant varieties</a> have long been developed and used. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flooding" title="Flooding">Flooding</a> is an issue that many rice growers face, especially in South and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_East_Asia" title="South East Asia">South East Asia</a> where flooding annually affects 20 million hectares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-176">[176]</a></sup> Standard rice varieties cannot withstand stagnant flooding of more than about a week,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> mainly as it disallows the plant access to necessary requirements such as sunlight and essential gas exchanges, inevitably leading to plants being unable to recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-176">[176]</a></sup>
In the past, this has led to massive losses in yields, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, where in 2006, rice crops worth $65 million were lost to flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-178">[178]</a></sup> Recently developed cultivars seek to improve flood tolerance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drought-tolerant_rice">Drought-tolerant rice</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">Drought</a> represents a significant environmental stress for rice production, with 19–23 million hectares of rainfed rice production in South and South East Asia often at risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irri.org_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-irri.org-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-180">[180]</a></sup> Under drought conditions, without sufficient water to afford them the ability to obtain the required levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrients" title="Nutrients">nutrients</a> from the soil, conventional commercial rice varieties can be severely affected – for example, yield losses as high as 40% have affected some parts of India, with resulting losses of around US$800 million annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> conducts research into developing drought-tolerant rice varieties, including the varieties 5411 and Sookha dhan, currently being employed by farmers in the Philippines and Nepal respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-180">[180]</a></sup> In addition, in 2013 the Japanese National Institute for Agrobiological Sciences led a team which successfully inserted the DEEPER ROOTING 1 (DRO1) gene, from the Philippine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upland_and_lowland_(freshwater_ecology)" title="Upland and lowland (freshwater ecology)">upland</a> rice variety Kinandang Patong, into the popular commercial rice variety IR64, giving rise to a far deeper root system in the resulting plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-181">[181]</a></sup> This facilitates an improved ability for the rice plant to derive its required nutrients in times of drought via accessing deeper layers of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a>, a feature demonstrated by trials which saw the IR64 + DRO1 rice yields drop by 10% under moderate drought conditions, compared to 60% for the unmodified IR64 variety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salt-tolerant_rice">Salt-tolerant rice</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Crop_tolerance_to_seawater" title="Crop tolerance to seawater">Crop tolerance to seawater</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">Soil salinity</a> poses a major threat to rice crop productivity, particularly along low-lying coastal areas during the dry season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irri.org_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-irri.org-179">[179]</a></sup> For example, roughly 1 million hectares of the coastal areas of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> are affected by saline soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> These high concentrations of salt can severely affect rice plants’ normal <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, especially during early stages of growth, and as such farmers are often forced to abandon these otherwise potentially usable areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Progress has been made, however, in developing rice varieties capable of tolerating such conditions; the hybrid created from the cross between the commercial rice variety IR56 and the wild rice species <i>Oryza coarctata</i> is one example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-186">[186]</a></sup> <i>O. coarctata</i> is capable of successful growth in soils with double the limit of salinity of normal varieties, but lacks the ability to produce edible rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-186">[186]</a></sup> Developed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> variety can utilise specialised leaf glands that allow for the removal of salt into the atmosphere. It was initially produced from one successful <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a> out of 34,000 crosses between the two species; this was then <a href="/wiki/Backcrossing" title="Backcrossing">backcrossed</a> to IR56 with the aim of preserving the genes responsible for salt tolerance that were inherited from <i>O. coarctata</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-184">[184]</a></sup> Extensive trials are planned prior to the new variety being available to farmers by approximately 2017–18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_egypt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="423" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Rice_egypt.png/250px-Rice_egypt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Rice_egypt.png/375px-Rice_egypt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Rice_egypt.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_egypt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The irrigated rice (paddy) crop in Egypt has a salt tolerance of ECe=5.5 dS/m beyond which the yield declines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>When the problem of <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">soil salinity</a> arises it will be opportune to select salt tolerant varieties (IRRI <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> or to resort to <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity_control" title="Soil salinity control">soil salinity control</a>.
</p><p>Soil salinity is often measured as the electric conductivity (EC) of the extract of a saturated soil paste (ECe). The EC units are usually expressed in millimho/cm or dS/m. The critical ECe value of 5.5 dS/m in the figure, obtained from measurements in farmers' fields, indicates that the rice crop is slightly salt sensitive.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment-friendly_rice">Environment-friendly rice</span></h3>
<p>Producing rice in <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddies</a> is harmful for the environment due to the release of methane by <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic bacteria</a>. These bacteria live in the anaerobic waterlogged soil, and live off nutrients released by rice roots. Researchers have recently reported in <i>Nature</i> that putting the <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> gene SUSIBA2 into rice creates a shift in biomass production from root to shoot (above ground tissue becomes larger, while below ground tissue is reduced), decreasing the methanogen population, and resulting in a reduction of methane emissions of up to 97%. Apart from this environmental benefit, the modification also increases the amount of rice grains by 43%, which makes it a useful tool in feeding a growing world population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meiosis_and_DNA_repair">Meiosis and DNA repair</span></h2>
<p>Rice is used as a model organism for investigating the molecular mechanisms of meiosis and <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> in higher plants. Meiosis is a key stage of the sexual cycle in which diploid cells in the <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovule</a> (female structure) and the <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">anther</a> (male structure) produce haploid cells that develop further into gametophytes and <a href="/wiki/Gamete#Plants" title="Gamete">gametes</a>. So far, 28 meiotic genes of rice have been characterized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Studies of rice gene OsRAD51C showed that this gene is necessary for <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombinational repair</a> of DNA, particularly the accurate repair of DNA double-strand breaks during meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Rice gene OsDMC1 was found to be essential for pairing of homologous chromosomes during meiosis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> and rice gene OsMRE11 was found to be required for both synapsis of homologous chromosomes and repair of double-strand breaks during meiosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_roles_of_rice">Cultural roles of rice</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="513" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Beeld_van_Dewi_Sri_de_rijstgodin_TMnr_60016918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient statue of <a href="/wiki/Dewi_Sri" title="Dewi Sri">Dewi Sri</a> from Java (c. 9th century)</div></div></div>
<p>Rice plays an important role in certain religions and popular beliefs. In many cultures relatives will scatter rice during or towards the end of a <a href="/wiki/Wedding" title="Wedding">wedding</a> ceremony in front of the bride and groom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pounded_rice_ritual" title="Pounded rice ritual">pounded rice ritual</a> is conducted during weddings in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. The bride gives a leafplate full of pounded rice to the groom after he requests it politely from her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> rice wine, popularly known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tapuy" title="Tapuy">tapuy</a></i>, is used for important occasions such as weddings, rice harvesting ceremonies and other celebrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dewi_Sri" title="Dewi Sri">Dewi Sri</a> is the traditional rice goddess of the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sundanese_people" title="Sundanese people">Sundanese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Balinese_people" title="Balinese people">Balinese people</a> in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. Most rituals involving Dewi Sri are associated with the mythical origin attributed to the rice plant, the staple food of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> a similar rice deity is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Phosop" title="Phosop">Phosop</a></i>; she is a deity more related to ancient local folklore than a goddess of a structured, mainstream religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> The same female rice deity is known as <i>Po Ino Nogar</i> in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and as <i>Nang Khosop</i> in <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>. Ritual offerings are made during the different stages of rice production to propitiate the Rice Goddess in the corresponding cultures.
</p><p>A 2014 study of <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> communities found that a history of farming rice makes cultures more psychologically interdependent, whereas a history of farming wheat makes cultures more independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ploughing_Ceremony" title="Royal Ploughing Ceremony">Royal Ploughing Ceremony</a> is held in certain Asian countries to mark the beginning of the rice planting season. It is still honored in the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>.
</p>
